<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Sexually transmitted infection</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Infection transmitted through human sexual behavior</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"STD" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/STD_(disambiguation)" title="STD (disambiguation)">STD (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Sexually transmitted infections" redirects here. For the journal, see <a href="/wiki/Sexually_Transmitted_Infections_(journal)" title="Sexually Transmitted Infections (journal)"><i>Sexually Transmitted Infections</i> (journal)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Sexual disease" redirects here. Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dysfunction" title="Sexual dysfunction">sexual dysfunction</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Medical condition</div><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" (medicine)">Complications</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Infertility" title="Infertility">Infertility</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-1">[1]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Causes</th><td class="infobox-data">Infections commonly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transmission_(medicine)" title="Transmission (medicine)">spread</a> by <a href="/wiki/Human_sexual_activity" title="Human sexual activity">sex</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-1">[1]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Prevention</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Sexual_abstinence" href="#cite_note-CDC2013P-2">[2]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Frequency</th><td class="infobox-data">1.1 billion (STIs other than HIV/AIDS, 2015)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBD2015Pre_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-GBD2015Pre-3">[3]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Deaths</th><td class="infobox-data">108,000 (STIs other than HIV/AIDS, 2015)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBD2015De_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-GBD2015De-4">[4]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Sexually transmitted infections</b> (<b>STIs</b>), also referred to as <b>sexually transmitted diseases</b> (<b>STDs</b>) and the older term <b>venereal diseases</b>, are <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infections</a> that are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transmission_(medicine)" title="Transmission (medicine)">spread</a> by <a href="/wiki/Human_sexual_activity" title="Human sexual activity">sexual activity</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Sexual_intercourse" title="Sexual intercourse">vaginal intercourse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anal_sex" title="Anal sex">anal sex</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oral_sex" title="Oral sex">oral sex</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Women2017_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Women2017-5">[5]</a></sup> STIs often do not initially cause symptoms,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-1">[1]</a></sup> which results in a risk of passing the infection on to others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Symptoms and signs of STIs may include <a href="/wiki/Vaginal_discharge" title="Vaginal discharge">vaginal discharge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Penile_discharge" title="Penile discharge">penile discharge</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genital_ulcers" title="Genital ulcers">ulcers on or around the genitals</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pelvic_pain" title="Pelvic pain">pelvic pain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-1">[1]</a></sup>  Some STIs can cause <a href="/wiki/Infertility" title="Infertility">infertility</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bacterial STIs include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chlamydia_infection" title="Chlamydia infection">chlamydia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gonorrhea" title="Gonorrhea">gonorrhea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">syphilis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-1">[1]</a></sup> Viral STIs include <a href="/wiki/Genital_herpes" title="Genital herpes">genital herpes</a>, <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV/AIDS</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genital_warts" title="Genital warts">genital warts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_1-8"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-1">[1]</a></sup> Parasitic STIs include <a href="/wiki/Trichomoniasis" title="Trichomoniasis">trichomoniasis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_1-9"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-1">[1]</a></sup>  STI diagnostic tests are usually easily available in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developed_world" title="Developed world">developed world</a>, but they are often unavailable in the developing world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_1-10"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vaccinations" title="Vaccinations">vaccinations</a> may also decrease the risk of certain infections including <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B_vaccine" title="Hepatitis B vaccine">hepatitis B</a> and some types of <a href="/wiki/HPV_vaccine" title="HPV vaccine">HPV</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2013P_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2013P-2">[2]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Safe_sex" title="Safe sex">Safe sex</a> practices, such as use of <a href="/wiki/Condom" title="Condom">condoms</a>, having a smaller number of sexual partners, and being in a relationship in which each person only has sex with the other also decreases the risk of STIs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_1-11"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2013P_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2013P-2">[2]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Comprehensive_sex_education" title="Comprehensive sex education">Comprehensive sex education</a> may also be useful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Most STIs are treatable and curable; of the most common infections, syphilis, gonorrhea, chlamydia, and trichomoniasis are curable, while HIV/AIDS and genital herpes are not curable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_1-12"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015, about 1.1 billion people had STIs other than HIV/AIDS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBD2015Pre_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-GBD2015Pre-3">[3]</a></sup> About 500 million were infected with either syphilis, gonorrhea, chlamydia or trichomoniasis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_1-13"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-1">[1]</a></sup> At least an additional 530 million people have genital herpes, and 290 million women have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_papillomavirus" title="Human papillomavirus">human papillomavirus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_1-14"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-1">[1]</a></sup> STIs other than HIV resulted in 108,000 deaths in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBD2015De_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-GBD2015De-4">[4]</a></sup> In the United States, there were 19 million new cases of STIs in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2012Cases_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2012Cases-9">[9]</a></sup> Historical documentation of STIs dates back to at least the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ebers_papyrus" title="Ebers papyrus">Ebers papyrus</a> around 1550 BC and the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is often shame and <a href="/wiki/Social_stigma" title="Social stigma">stigma</a> associated with STIs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_1-15"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-1">[1]</a></sup> The term <i>sexually transmitted infection</i> is generally preferred over <i>sexually transmitted disease</i> or <i>venereal disease</i>, as it includes those who do not have symptomatic <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2003Term_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2003Term-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>

<p>Not all STIs are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symptom" title="Symptom">symptomatic</a>, and symptoms may not appear immediately after infection. In some instances a disease can be carried with no symptoms, which leaves a greater risk of passing the disease on to others. Depending on the disease, some untreated STIs can lead to <a href="/wiki/Infertility" title="Infertility">infertility</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chronic_pain" title="Chronic pain">chronic pain</a> or death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-STI_video_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-STI_video-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>The presence of an STI in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prepubescent" title="Prepubescent">prepubescent</a> children may indicate <a href="/wiki/Sexual_abuse" title="Sexual abuse">sexual abuse</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman2012_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffman2012-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cause">Cause</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transmission">Transmission</span></h3>
<p>A sexually transmitted infection present in a pregnant woman may be passed on to the infant before or after birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bacterial">Bacterial</span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Chancroid" title="Chancroid">Chancroid</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Haemophilus_ducreyi" title="Haemophilus ducreyi">Haemophilus ducreyi</a></i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chlamydia_infection" title="Chlamydia infection">Chlamydia</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Chlamydia_trachomatis" title="Chlamydia trachomatis">Chlamydia trachomatis</a></i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gonorrhea" title="Gonorrhea">Gonorrhea</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Neisseria_gonorrhoeae" title="Neisseria gonorrhoeae">Neisseria gonorrhoeae</a></i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Granuloma_inguinale" title="Granuloma inguinale">Granuloma inguinale</a> or (<i><a href="/wiki/Klebsiella_granulomatis" title="Klebsiella granulomatis">Klebsiella granulomatis</a></i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Mycoplasma_genitalium" title="Mycoplasma genitalium">Mycoplasma genitalium</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ljubin-SternakMeštrović2014_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ljubin-SternakMeštrović2014-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZareiRezania2013_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-ZareiRezania2013-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ManchesterMcGowin2011_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-ManchesterMcGowin2011-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Mycoplasma_hominis" title="Mycoplasma hominis">Mycoplasma hominis</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CainiGandini2014_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-CainiGandini2014-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ljubin-SternakMeštrović2014_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ljubin-SternakMeštrović2014-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchlichtLovrich2004_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-SchlichtLovrich2004-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McIverRismanto2009_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-McIverRismanto2009-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">Syphilis</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Treponema_pallidum" title="Treponema pallidum">Treponema pallidum</a></i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ureaplasma_infection" title="Ureaplasma infection">Ureaplasma infection</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ClarkTal2014_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-ClarkTal2014-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LarsenHwang2010_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-LarsenHwang2010-37">[37]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Viral">Viral</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herpes_simplex_virus_pap_test.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2028" data-file-width="2620" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Herpes_simplex_virus_pap_test.jpg/220px-Herpes_simplex_virus_pap_test.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Herpes_simplex_virus_pap_test.jpg/330px-Herpes_simplex_virus_pap_test.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Herpes_simplex_virus_pap_test.jpg/440px-Herpes_simplex_virus_pap_test.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Herpes_simplex_virus_pap_test.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Micrograph" title="Micrograph">Micrograph</a> showing the viral <a href="/wiki/Cytopathic_effect" title="Cytopathic effect">cytopathic effect</a> of herpes (ground glass nuclear inclusions, multi-nucleation). <a href="/wiki/Pap_test" title="Pap test">Pap test</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pap_stain" title="Pap stain">Pap stain</a>.</div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Viral_hepatitis" title="Viral hepatitis">Viral hepatitis</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B_virus" title="Hepatitis B virus">hepatitis B virus</a>)—saliva, venereal fluids.<br/>(Note: <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_A" title="Hepatitis A">hepatitis A</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_E" title="Hepatitis E">hepatitis E</a> are transmitted via the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fecal-oral_route" title="Fecal-oral route">fecal-oral route</a>; <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_C" title="Hepatitis C">hepatitis C</a> is rarely sexually transmittable,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Workowski_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Workowski-50">[50]</a></sup> and the route of transmission of <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_D" title="Hepatitis D">hepatitis D</a> (only if infected with B) is uncertain, but may include sexual transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Herpes_simplex" title="Herpes simplex">Herpes simplex</a> (<a href="/wiki/Herpes_simplex_virus" title="Herpes simplex virus">Herpes simplex virus</a> 1, 2) skin and mucosal, transmissible with or without visible blisters</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> (<i>Human Immunodeficiency Virus</i>)—venereal fluids, semen, breast milk, blood</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Human_papillomavirus_infection" title="Human papillomavirus infection">HPV</a> (<i>Human Papillomavirus</i>)—skin and mucosal contact. 'High risk' types of HPV cause almost all <a href="/wiki/Cervical_cancer" title="Cervical cancer">cervical cancers</a>, as well as some <a href="/wiki/Anal_cancer" title="Anal cancer">anal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Penile_cancer" title="Penile cancer">penile</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vulvar_cancer" title="Vulvar cancer">vulvar cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Some other types of HPV cause <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genital_warts" title="Genital warts">genital warts</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Molluscum_contagiosum" title="Molluscum contagiosum">Molluscum contagiosum</a> (<a href="/wiki/Molluscum_contagiosum_virus" title="Molluscum contagiosum virus">molluscum contagiosum virus</a> MCV)—close contact<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Zika_virus" title="Zika virus">Zika virus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parasites">Parasites</span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Crab_louse" title="Crab louse">Crab louse</a>, colloquially known as "crabs" or "pubic lice" (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pthirus_pubis" title="Pthirus pubis">Pthirus pubis</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The infestation and accompanying inflammation is <a href="/wiki/Pediculosis_pubis" title="Pediculosis pubis">Pediculosis pubis</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Scabies" title="Scabies">Scabies</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Sarcoptes_scabiei" title="Sarcoptes scabiei">Sarcoptes scabiei</a></i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Trichomoniasis" title="Trichomoniasis">Trichomoniasis</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Trichomonas_vaginalis" title="Trichomonas vaginalis">Trichomonas vaginalis</a></i>), colloquially known as "trich"</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Main_types">Main types</span></h3>
<p>Sexually transmitted infections include:
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chlamydia_infection" title="Chlamydia infection">Chlamydia</a> is a sexually transmitted infection caused by the bacterium <i><a href="/wiki/Chlamydia_trachomatis" title="Chlamydia trachomatis">Chlamydia trachomatis</a></i>. In women, symptoms may include abnormal vaginal discharge, burning during urination, and bleeding in between periods, although most women do not experience any symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King,_B._2009_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-King,_B._2009-60">[60]</a></sup> Symptoms in men include pain when urinating, and abnormal discharge from their penis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> If left untreated in both men and women, chlamydia can infect the urinary tract and potentially lead to <a href="/wiki/Pelvic_inflammatory_disease" title="Pelvic inflammatory disease">pelvic inflammatory disease</a> (PID). PID can cause serious problems during pregnancy and even has the potential to cause infertility. It can cause a woman to have a potentially deadly <a href="/wiki/Ectopic_pregnancy" title="Ectopic pregnancy">ectopic pregnancy</a>, in which the egg implants outside of the uterus. However, chlamydia can be cured with antibiotics.</li>
<li>The two most common forms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herpes" title="Herpes">herpes</a> are caused by infection with <a href="/wiki/Herpes_simplex_virus" title="Herpes simplex virus">herpes simplex virus</a> (HSV). HSV-1 is typically acquired orally and causes cold sores, HSV-2 is usually acquired during sexual contact and affects the genitals, however, either strain may affect either site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Some people are asymptomatic or have very mild symptoms. Those that do experience symptoms usually notice them 2 to 20 days after exposure which lasts 2 to 4 weeks. Symptoms can include small fluid-filled blisters, headaches, backaches, itching or tingling sensations in the genital or anal area, pain during urination, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flu_like_symptoms" title="Flu like symptoms">flu like symptoms</a>, swollen glands, or fever. Herpes is spread through skin contact with a person infected with the virus. The virus affects the areas where it entered the body. This can occur through kissing, vaginal intercourse, oral sex or anal sex. The virus is most infectious during times when there are visible symptoms, however, those who are asymptomatic can still spread the virus through skin contact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The initial infection and symptoms are usually the most severe because the body does not have any antibodies built up. After the primary attack, one might have recurring attacks that are milder or might not even have future attacks. There is no cure for the disease but there are antiviral medications that treat its symptoms and lower the risk of transmission (<a href="/wiki/Valaciclovir" title="Valaciclovir">Valtrex</a>). Although HSV-1 is typically the "oral" version of the virus, and HSV-2 is typically the "genital" version of the virus, a person with HSV-1 orally CAN transmit that virus to their partner genitally. The virus, either type, will settle into a nerve bundle either at the top of the spine, producing the "oral" outbreak, or a second nerve bundle at the base of the spine, producing the genital outbreak.</li>
<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_papillomavirus" title="Human papillomavirus">human papillomavirus</a> (HPV) is the most common STI in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> There are more than 40 different strands of HPV and many do not cause any health problems. In 90% of cases, the body's immune system clears the infection naturally within two years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Some cases may not be cleared and can lead to genital warts (bumps around the genitals that can be small or large, raised or flat, or shaped like cauliflower) or cervical cancer and other HPV related cancers. Symptoms might not show up until advanced stages. It is important for women to get pap smears in order to check for and treat cancers. There are also two vaccines available for women (<a href="/wiki/Cervarix" title="Cervarix">Cervarix</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gardasil" title="Gardasil">Gardasil</a>) that protect against the types of HPV that cause cervical cancer. HPV can be passed through genital-to-genital contact as well as during oral sex. The infected partner might not have any symptoms.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gonorrhea" title="Gonorrhea">Gonorrhea</a> is caused by bacterium that lives on moist mucous membranes in the urethra, vagina, rectum, mouth, throat, and eyes. The infection can spread through contact with the penis, vagina, mouth, or anus. Symptoms of gonorrhea usually appear two to five days after contact with an infected partner however, some men might not notice symptoms for up to a month. Symptoms in men include burning and pain while urinating, increased urinary frequency, discharge from the penis (white, green, or yellow in color), red or swollen urethra, swollen or tender testicles, or sore throat. Symptoms in women may include vaginal discharge, burning or itching while urinating, painful sexual intercourse, severe pain in lower abdomen (if infection spreads to fallopian tubes), or fever (if infection spreads to fallopian tubes); however, many women do not show any symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Antibiotic resistant strains of Gonorrhea are a significant concern, but most cases can be cured with existing antibiotics.</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SyphilistischeZiekten.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1136" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/SyphilistischeZiekten.jpg/220px-SyphilistischeZiekten.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/SyphilistischeZiekten.jpg/330px-SyphilistischeZiekten.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/SyphilistischeZiekten.jpg/440px-SyphilistischeZiekten.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SyphilistischeZiekten.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Secondary syphilis</div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">Syphilis</a> is an STI caused by a bacterium. Untreated, it can lead to complications and death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Clinical manifestations of syphilis include the ulceration of the uro-genital tract, mouth or rectum; if left untreated the symptoms worsen. In recent years, the prevalence of syphilis has declined in Western Europe, but it has increased in Eastern Europe (former Soviet states). A high incidence of syphilis can be found in places such as <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>, Papua New Guinea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Syphilis infections are increasing in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ClementOkeke2014_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-ClementOkeke2014-69">[69]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Trichomoniasis" title="Trichomoniasis">Trichomoniasis</a> is a common STI that is caused by infection with a protozoan parasite called <i>Trichomonas vaginalis</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Trichomoniasis affects both women and men, but symptoms are more common in women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Most patients are treated with an antibiotic called metronidazole, which is very effective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> (human immunodeficiency virus) damages the body's immune system, which interferes with its ability to fight off disease-causing agents. The virus kills <a href="/wiki/CD4" title="CD4">CD4</a> cells, which are white blood cells that help fight off various infections. HIV is carried in body fluids and is spread by sexual activity. It can also be spread by contact with infected blood, breastfeeding, childbirth, and from mother to child during pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> When HIV is at its most advanced stage, an individual is said to have <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">AIDS</a> (acquired immunodeficiency syndrome).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> There are different stages of the progression of and HIV infection. The stages include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acute_HIV_Infection" title="Acute HIV Infection">primary infection</a>, asymptomatic infection, symptomatic infection, and AIDS. In the primary infection stage, an individual will have flu-like symptoms (headache, fatigue, fever, muscle aches) for about two weeks. In the asymptomatic stage, symptoms usually disappear, and the patient can remain asymptomatic for years. When HIV progresses to the symptomatic stage, the immune system is weakened and has a low cell count of CD4+ <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T_Cells" title="T Cells">T cells</a>. When the HIV infection becomes life-threatening, it is called AIDS. People with AIDS fall prey to opportunistic infections and die as a result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King,_B._2009_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-King,_B._2009-60">[60]</a></sup> When the disease was first discovered in the 1980s, those who had AIDS were not likely to live longer than a few years. There are now antiretroviral drugs (ARVs) available to treat HIV infections. There is no known cure for HIV or AIDS but the drugs help suppress the virus. By suppressing the amount of virus in the body, people can lead longer and healthier lives. Even though their virus levels may be low they can still spread the virus to others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Viruses_in_semen">Viruses in semen</span></h3>
<p>Twenty-seven different viruses have been identified in semen. Information on whether or not transmission occurs or whether the viruses cause disease is uncertain. Some of these microbes are known to be sexually transmitted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid29048276_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid29048276-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pathophysiology">Pathophysiology</span></h2>
<p>Many STIs are (more easily) transmitted through the <a href="/wiki/Mucous_membrane" title="Mucous membrane">mucous membranes</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Penis" title="Penis">penis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vulva" title="Vulva">vulva</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rectum" title="Rectum">rectum</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urinary_tract" title="Urinary tract">urinary tract</a> and (less often—depending on type of infection) the <a href="/wiki/Mouth" title="Mouth">mouth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Throat" title="Throat">throat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_tract" title="Respiratory tract">respiratory tract</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_eyes" title="Human eyes">eyes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> The visible membrane covering the <a href="/wiki/Glans_penis" title="Glans penis">head of the penis</a> is a mucous membrane, though it produces no <a href="/wiki/Mucus" title="Mucus">mucus</a> (similar to the <a href="/wiki/Lip" title="Lip">lips</a> of the mouth). Mucous membranes differ from <a href="/wiki/Skin" title="Skin">skin</a> in that they allow certain pathogens into the body. The amount of contact with infective sources which causes infection varies with each pathogen but in all cases, a disease may result from even light contact from fluid carriers like venereal fluids onto a mucous membrane.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Some STIs such as HIV can be transmitted from mother to child either during pregnancy or breastfeeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
Healthcare professionals suggest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Safer_sex" title="Safer sex">safer sex</a>, such as the use of <a href="/wiki/Condom" title="Condom">condoms</a>, as a reliable way of decreasing the risk of contracting sexually transmitted diseases during sexual activity, but safer sex cannot be considered to provide complete protection from an STI. The transfer of and exposure to bodily fluids, such as <a href="/wiki/Blood_transfusion" title="Blood transfusion">blood transfusions</a> and other blood products, sharing injection <a href="/wiki/Intravenous_therapy" title="Intravenous therapy">needles</a>, needle-stick injuries (when medical staff are inadvertently jabbed or pricked with needles during medical procedures), sharing <a href="/wiki/Tattoo" title="Tattoo">tattoo</a> needles, and childbirth are other avenues of transmission. These different means put certain groups, such as medical workers, and <a href="/wiki/Haemophilia" title="Haemophilia">haemophiliacs</a> and drug users, particularly at risk.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>It is possible to be an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symptom" title="Symptom">asymptomatic</a> carrier of sexually transmitted diseases. In particular, sexually transmitted diseases in women often cause the serious condition of pelvic inflammatory disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:STILL_AT_THE_TOP_OF_THE_SICK_LIST_-_NARA_-_515490.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2380" decoding="async" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/STILL_AT_THE_TOP_OF_THE_SICK_LIST_-_NARA_-_515490.tif/lossy-page1-220px-STILL_AT_THE_TOP_OF_THE_SICK_LIST_-_NARA_-_515490.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/STILL_AT_THE_TOP_OF_THE_SICK_LIST_-_NARA_-_515490.tif/lossy-page1-330px-STILL_AT_THE_TOP_OF_THE_SICK_LIST_-_NARA_-_515490.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/STILL_AT_THE_TOP_OF_THE_SICK_LIST_-_NARA_-_515490.tif/lossy-page1-440px-STILL_AT_THE_TOP_OF_THE_SICK_LIST_-_NARA_-_515490.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:STILL_AT_THE_TOP_OF_THE_SICK_LIST_-_NARA_-_515490.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> US Army poster warning of venereal disease</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Medical_test" title="Medical test">Testing</a> may be for a single infection, or consist of a number of tests for a range of STIs, including tests for <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">syphilis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trichomonas" title="Trichomonas">trichomonas</a>, gonorrhea, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chlamydia_infection" title="Chlamydia infection">chlamydia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herpes" title="Herpes">herpes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis" title="Hepatitis">hepatitis</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HIV_test" title="HIV test">HIV</a>. No procedure tests for all infectious agents.
</p><p>STI tests may be used for a number of reasons:
</p>
<ul><li>as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diagnostic_test" title="Diagnostic test">diagnostic test</a> to determine the cause of symptoms or illness</li>
<li>as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Screening_test" title="Screening test">screening test</a> to detect asymptomatic or presymptomatic <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infections</a></li>
<li>as a check that prospective sexual partners are free of disease before they engage in sex without <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Safer_sex" title="Safer sex">safer sex</a> precautions (for example, when starting a long term mutually monogamous sexual relationship, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluid_bonding_(sexual_practice)" title="Fluid bonding (sexual practice)">fluid bonding</a>, or for procreation).</li>
<li>as a check prior to or during pregnancy, to prevent harm to the baby</li>
<li>as a check after birth, to check that the baby has not caught an STI from the mother</li>
<li>to prevent the use of infected <a href="/wiki/Blood_donation" title="Blood donation">donated blood</a> or <a href="/wiki/Organ_donation" title="Organ donation">organs</a></li>
<li>as part of the process of <a href="/wiki/Contact_tracing" title="Contact tracing">contact tracing</a> from a known infected individual</li>
<li>as part of mass <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epidemiological" title="Epidemiological">epidemiological</a> surveillance</li></ul>
<p>Early identification and treatment results in less chance to spread disease, and for some conditions may improve the outcomes of treatment. There is often a <a href="/wiki/Window_period" title="Window period">window period</a> after initial infection during which an STI test will be negative. During this period, the infection may be transmissible. The duration of this period varies depending on the infection and the test. Diagnosis may also be delayed by reluctance of the infected person to seek a medical professional. One report indicated that people turn to the Internet rather than to a medical professional for information on STIs to a higher degree than for other sexual problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quilliam2011_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quilliam2011-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%22VD_CAN_BE_CURED_BUT_THERE%27S_NO_MEDICINE_FOR_REGRET%22_-_NARA_-_515957_-_retouched.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="9144" data-file-width="6819" decoding="async" height="295" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/%22VD_CAN_BE_CURED_BUT_THERE%27S_NO_MEDICINE_FOR_REGRET%22_-_NARA_-_515957_-_retouched.jpg/220px-%22VD_CAN_BE_CURED_BUT_THERE%27S_NO_MEDICINE_FOR_REGRET%22_-_NARA_-_515957_-_retouched.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/%22VD_CAN_BE_CURED_BUT_THERE%27S_NO_MEDICINE_FOR_REGRET%22_-_NARA_-_515957_-_retouched.jpg/330px-%22VD_CAN_BE_CURED_BUT_THERE%27S_NO_MEDICINE_FOR_REGRET%22_-_NARA_-_515957_-_retouched.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/%22VD_CAN_BE_CURED_BUT_THERE%27S_NO_MEDICINE_FOR_REGRET%22_-_NARA_-_515957_-_retouched.jpg/440px-%22VD_CAN_BE_CURED_BUT_THERE%27S_NO_MEDICINE_FOR_REGRET%22_-_NARA_-_515957_-_retouched.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%22VD_CAN_BE_CURED_BUT_THERE%27S_NO_MEDICINE_FOR_REGRET%22_-_NARA_-_515957_-_retouched.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A poster from the <a href="/wiki/Office_for_Emergency_Management" title="Office for Emergency Management">Office for Emergency Management</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Office_of_War_Information" title="Office of War Information">Office of War Information</a>, 1941–1945</div></div></div>
<p>Until the 1990s,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> STIs were commonly known as <i>venereal diseases</i>, an antiquated <a href="/wiki/Euphemism" title="Euphemism">euphemism</a> derived from the Latin <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">venereus</i></span>, being the adjectival form of <a href="/wiki/Venus_(mythology)" title="Venus (mythology)">Venus</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">Roman goddess</a> of love.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Venereal_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Venereal-82">[82]</a></sup> However, in the post-classical education era the euphemistic effect was entirely lost, and the common abbreviation "VD" held only negative connotations. Other former euphemisms for STIs include "blood diseases" and "social diseases".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllanBurridge1991_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllanBurridge1991-83">[83]</a></sup> The present euphemism is in the use of the initials "STI" rather than in the words they represent. The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) has recommended the more inclusive term <i>sexually transmitted infection</i> since 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2003Term_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2003Term-11">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">Public health</a> officials originally introduced the term <i>sexually transmitted infection</i>, which clinicians are increasingly using alongside the term <i>sexually transmitted disease</i> in order to distinguish it from the former.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Safe_sex" title="Safe sex">Safe sex</a></div>
<p>Strategies for reducing STI risk include: vaccination, <a href="/wiki/Mutual_monogamy" title="Mutual monogamy">mutual monogamy</a>, reducing the number of sexual partners, and abstinence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Behavioral counseling for all sexually active adolescents and for adults who are at increased risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Such interactive counseling, which can be resource-intensive, is directed at a person's risk, the situations in which risk occurs, and the use of personalized goal-setting strategies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cdc2015_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-cdc2015-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>
The most effective way to prevent sexual transmission of STIs is to avoid contact of body parts or fluids which can lead to transfer with an infected partner. Not all sexual activities involve contact: <a href="/wiki/Cybersex" title="Cybersex">cybersex</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phone_sex" title="Phone sex">phone sex</a> or <a href="/wiki/Masturbation" title="Masturbation">masturbation</a> from a distance are methods of avoiding contact. Proper use of condoms reduces contact and risk. Although a condom is effective in limiting exposure, some disease transmission may occur even with a condom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Syphilis_is_a_dangerous_disease.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="978" decoding="async" height="346" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Syphilis_is_a_dangerous_disease.png/220px-Syphilis_is_a_dangerous_disease.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Syphilis_is_a_dangerous_disease.png/330px-Syphilis_is_a_dangerous_disease.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Syphilis_is_a_dangerous_disease.png/440px-Syphilis_is_a_dangerous_disease.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Syphilis_is_a_dangerous_disease.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Syphilis is a dangerous disease, but it can be cured". Poster encouraging treatment. Published between 1936 and 1938.</div></div></div><p>Both partners can get tested for STIs before initiating sexual contact, or before resuming contact if a partner engaged in contact with someone else. Many infections are not detectable immediately after exposure, so enough time must be allowed between possible exposures and testing for the tests to be accurate. Certain STIs, particularly certain persistent viruses like HPV, may be impossible to detect.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources_(medicine)" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources (medicine)"><span title="Material near this tag needs references to reliable medical sources. (December 2017)">medical citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Some treatment facilities utilize in-home test kits and have the person return the test for follow-up. Other facilities strongly encourage that those previously infected return to ensure that the infection has been eliminated. Novel strategies to foster re-testing have been the use of text messaging and email as reminders. These types of reminders are now used in addition to phone calls and letters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DesaiWoodhall2015_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-DesaiWoodhall2015-88">[88]</a></sup> After obtaining a sexual history, a healthcare provider can encourage risk reduction by providing prevention <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Counseling" title="Counseling">counseling</a>. Prevention counseling is most effective if provided in a nonjudgmental and empathetic manner appropriate to the person's culture, language, gender, sexual orientation, age, and developmental level. Prevention counseling for STIs is usually offered to all sexually active adolescents and to all adults who have received a diagnosis, have had an STI in the past year, or have <a href="/wiki/Multiple_sex_partners" title="Multiple sex partners">multiple sex partners</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cdc2015_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-cdc2015-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vaccines">Vaccines</span></h3>
<p>Vaccines are available that protect against some viral STIs, such as <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_A_vaccine" title="Hepatitis A vaccine">hepatitis A</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B_vaccine" title="Hepatitis B vaccine">hepatitis B</a>, and some types of <a href="/wiki/HPV_vaccine" title="HPV vaccine">HPV</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Vaccination before initiation of sexual contact is advised to assure maximal protection. The development of vaccines to protect against gonorrhea is ongoing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BaardaSikora2015_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-BaardaSikora2015-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Condoms">Condoms</span></h3>
<p>Condoms and <a href="/wiki/Female_condom" title="Female condom">female condoms</a> only provide protection when used properly as a barrier, and only to and from the area that they cover. Uncovered areas are still susceptible to many STIs.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In the case of HIV, sexual transmission routes almost always involve the penis, as HIV cannot spread through unbroken skin; therefore, properly shielding the penis with a properly worn condom from the vagina or anus effectively stops HIV transmission. An infected fluid to broken skin borne direct transmission of HIV would not be considered "sexually transmitted", but can still theoretically occur during sexual contact. This can be avoided simply by not engaging in sexual contact when presenting open, bleeding wounds.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Other STIs, even viral infections, can be prevented with the use of latex, polyurethane or polyisoprene condoms as a barrier. Some microorganisms and viruses are small enough to pass through the pores in natural skin condoms but are still too large to pass through latex or synthetic condoms.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Proper male condom usage entails:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Not putting the condom on too tight at the tip by leaving 1.5 centimetres (0.6 in) room for <a href="/wiki/Ejaculation" title="Ejaculation">ejaculation</a>. Putting the condom on too tightly can and often does lead to failure.</li>
<li>Wearing a condom too loose can defeat the barrier</li>
<li>Avoiding inverting or spilling a condom once worn, whether it has ejaculate in it or not</li>
<li>If a user attempts to unroll the condom, but realizes they have it on the wrong side, then this condom may not be effective</li>
<li>Being careful with the condom if handling it with long nails</li>
<li>Avoiding the use of oil-based lubricants (or anything with oil in it) with latex condoms, as oil can eat holes into them</li>
<li>Using flavored condoms for oral sex only, as the sugar in the flavoring can lead to yeast infections if used to penetrate</li></ul>
<p>In order to best protect oneself and the partner from STIs, the old condom and its contents are to be treated as infectious and properly disposed of. A new condom is used for each act of intercourse, as multiple usages increase the chance of breakage, defeating the effectiveness as a barrier.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In the case of female condoms, the device consists of two rings, one in each terminal portion. The larger ring should fit snugly over the cervix and the smaller ring remains outside the vagina, covering the vulva. This system provides some protection of the external genitalia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-femenine_condon_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-femenine_condon-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Cervical_cap" title="Cervical cap">cap</a> was developed after the cervical <a href="/wiki/Diaphragm_(birth_control)" title="Diaphragm (birth control)">diaphragm</a>. Both cover the cervix and the main difference between the diaphragm and the cap is that the latter must be used only once, using a new one in each sexual act. The diaphragm, however, can be used more than once. These two devices partially protect against STIs (they do not protect against HIV).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>Researchers had hoped that <a href="/wiki/Nonoxynol-9" title="Nonoxynol-9">nonoxynol-9</a>, a vaginal microbicide would help decrease STI risk.  Trials, however, have found it ineffective<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> and it may put women at a higher risk of HIV infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> There is evidence that vaginal <a href="/wiki/Dapivirine" title="Dapivirine">dapivirine</a> probably reduces HIV in women who have sex with men, other types of vaginal microbicides have not demonstrated effectiveness for HIV or STI's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is little evidence that school-based interventions such as sexual and reproductive health education programmes on contraceptive choices and condoms are effective on improving the sexual and reproductive health of adolescents. Incentive-based programmes may reduce adolescent pregnancy but more data is needed to confirm this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Screening">Screening</span></h2>
<p>Specific age groups, persons who participate in risky sexual behavior, or those have certain health conditions may require screening. The CDC recommends that sexually active women under the age of 25 and those over 25 at risk should be screened for chlamydia and gonorrhea yearly. Appropriate times for screening are during regular pelvic examinations and preconception evaluations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gavin2014_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gavin2014-97">[97]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid_amplification_tests" title="Nucleic acid amplification tests">Nucleic acid amplification tests</a> are the recommended method of diagnosis for gonorrhea and chlamydia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USPSTF2014_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-USPSTF2014-98">[98]</a></sup> This can be done on either urine in both men and women, vaginal or cervical swabs in women, or urethral swabs in men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USPSTF2014_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-USPSTF2014-98">[98]</a></sup> Screening can be performed:
</p>
<ul><li>to assess the presence of infection and prevent tubal infertility in women</li>
<li>during the initial evaluation before infertility treatment</li>
<li>to identify HIV infection</li>
<li>for men who have sex with men</li>
<li>for those who may have been exposed to hepatitis C</li>
<li>for HCV<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USPSTF2014_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-USPSTF2014-98">[98]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Management">Management</span></h2>
<p>In the case of rape, the person can be treated prophylacticly with antibiotics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mci2017_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-mci2017-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>An option for treating partners of patients (<a href="/wiki/Index_case" title="Index case">index cases</a>) diagnosed with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chlamydia_infection" title="Chlamydia infection">chlamydia</a> or gonorrhea is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patient-delivered_partner_therapy" title="Patient-delivered partner therapy">patient-delivered partner therapy</a>, which is the clinical practice of treating the sex partners of index cases by providing prescriptions or medications to the patient to take to his/her partner without the health care provider first examining the partner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPTFinalReport2006_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPTFinalReport2006-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (December 2017)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_sexually_transmitted_infections_by_prevalence" title="List of sexually transmitted infections by prevalence">List of sexually transmitted infections by prevalence</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:STDs_excluding_HIV_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="940" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/STDs_excluding_HIV_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/300px-STDs_excluding_HIV_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/STDs_excluding_HIV_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/450px-STDs_excluding_HIV_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/STDs_excluding_HIV_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/600px-STDs_excluding_HIV_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:STDs_excluding_HIV_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Age-standardized, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disability-adjusted_life_years" title="Disability-adjusted life years">disability-adjusted life years</a> for STDs (<i>excluding HIV</i>) per 100,000 inhabitants in 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> <div> <table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffa020; color:black;"> </span> 2–3</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff9a20; color:black;"> </span> 4–9</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#f08015; color:black;"> </span> 10–18</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#e06815; color:black;"> </span> 19–31</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#d85010; color:black;"> </span> 32–55</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#d02010; color:white;"> </span> 56–139</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>In 2008, it was estimated that 500 million people were infected with either syphilis, gonorrhea, chlamydia or trichomoniasis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_1-16"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-1">[1]</a></sup> At least an additional 530 million people have genital herpes and 290 million women have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_papillomavirus" title="Human papillomavirus">human papillomavirus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_1-17"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-1">[1]</a></sup> STIs other than HIV resulted in 142,000 deaths in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBD2013_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-GBD2013-102">[102]</a></sup> In the United States there were 19 million new cases of sexually transmitted infections in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2012Cases_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2012Cases-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2010, 19 million new cases of sexually transmitted infections occurred in women in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Women2017_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Women2017-5">[5]</a></sup> A 2008 <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">CDC</a> study found that 25–40% of U.S. teenage girls has a sexually transmitted disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Out of a population of almost 295,270,000 people<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> there were 110 million new and existing cases of eight sexually transmitted infections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>Over 400,000 sexually transmitted infections were reported in England in 2017, about the same as in 2016, but there were more than 20% increases in confirmed cases of gonorrhoea and syphilis. Since 2008 syphilis cases have risen by 148%, from 2,874 to 7,137, mostly among men who have sex with men.  The number of first cases of genital warts in 2017 among girls aged 15–17 years was just 441, 90% less than in 2009 – attributed to the national human papilloma virus immunisation programme.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AIDS" title="AIDS">AIDS</a> is among the leading causes of death in present-day <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> HIV/AIDS is transmitted primarily via unprotected sexual intercourse. More than 1.1 million persons are living with <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS_in_the_United_States" title="HIV/AIDS in the United States">HIV/AIDS in the United States</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> and it disproportionately impacts <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African Americans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B" title="Hepatitis B">Hepatitis B</a> is also considered a sexually transmitted disease because it can be spread through sexual contact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> The highest rates are found in Asia and Africa and lower rates are in the Americas and Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Approximately two billion people worldwide have been infected with the hepatitis B virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Here_Comes_the_Bride_Art.IWMPST14092.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="741" data-file-width="463" decoding="async" height="352" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Here_Comes_the_Bride_Art.IWMPST14092.jpg/220px-Here_Comes_the_Bride_Art.IWMPST14092.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Here_Comes_the_Bride_Art.IWMPST14092.jpg/330px-Here_Comes_the_Bride_Art.IWMPST14092.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Here_Comes_the_Bride_Art.IWMPST14092.jpg/440px-Here_Comes_the_Bride_Art.IWMPST14092.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Here_Comes_the_Bride_Art.IWMPST14092.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World War II-era British poster urging men to be tested for venereal diseases before marriage</div></div></div>
<p>The first well-recorded European outbreak of what is now known as syphilis occurred in 1494 when it broke out among French troops besieging <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_War_of_1494%E2%80%9398" title="Italian War of 1494–98">Italian War of 1494–98</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scars_of_Venus_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scars_of_Venus-114">[114]</a></sup> The disease may have originated from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbian_Exchange" title="Columbian Exchange">Columbian Exchange</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> From Naples, the disease swept across <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, killing more than five million people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Jared_Diamond" title="Jared Diamond">Jared Diamond</a> describes it, "[W]hen syphilis was first definitely recorded in Europe in 1495, its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pustule" title="Pustule">pustules</a> often covered the body from the head to the knees, caused flesh to fall from people's faces, and led to death within a few months," rendering it far more <a href="/wiki/Case_fatality_rate" title="Case fatality rate">fatal</a> than it is today. Diamond concludes,"[B]y 1546, the disease had evolved into the disease with the symptoms so well known to us today."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diamond1_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Diamond1-117">[117]</a></sup> Gonorrhea is recorded at least up to 700 years ago and associated with a district in Paris formerly known as "Le Clapiers". This is where the prostitutes were to be found at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BaardaSikora2015_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-BaardaSikora2015-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SheMayLookCleanBut.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3168" data-file-width="2176" decoding="async" height="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/SheMayLookCleanBut.jpg/220px-SheMayLookCleanBut.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/SheMayLookCleanBut.jpg/330px-SheMayLookCleanBut.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/SheMayLookCleanBut.jpg/440px-SheMayLookCleanBut.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SheMayLookCleanBut.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. propaganda poster targeted at <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> servicemen appealed to their patriotism in urging them to protect themselves. The text at the bottom of the poster reads, "You can't beat the <a href="/wiki/Axis_powers" title="Axis powers">Axis</a> if you get VD".</div></div></div>
<p>Prior to the invention of modern medicines, sexually transmitted diseases were generally incurable, and treatment was limited to treating the symptoms of the disease. The first voluntary hospital for venereal diseases was founded in 1746 at <a href="/wiki/London_Lock_Hospital" title="London Lock Hospital">London Lock Hospital</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AIM-Albert_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-AIM-Albert-118">[118]</a></sup> Treatment was not always voluntary: in the second half of the 19th century, the <a href="/wiki/Contagious_Diseases_Acts" title="Contagious Diseases Acts">Contagious Diseases Acts</a> were used to arrest suspected prostitutes. In 1924, a number of states concluded the <a href="/wiki/Brussels_Agreement_(1924)" title="Brussels Agreement (1924)">Brussels Agreement</a>, whereby states agreed to provide free or low-cost medical treatment at ports for merchant seamen with venereal diseases. A proponent of these approaches was Dr. <a href="/wiki/Nora_Wattie" title="Nora Wattie">Nora Wattie</a>, OBE, Venereal Diseases Officer in Glasgow from 1929, encouraged <a href="/wiki/Contact_tracing" title="Contact tracing">contact tracing</a> and volunteering for treatment, rather than the prevailing more judgemental view and published her own research on improving sex education and maternity care.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first effective treatment for a sexually transmitted disease was <a href="/wiki/Arsphenamine" title="Arsphenamine">salvarsan</a>, a treatment for syphilis. With the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a>, a large number of sexually transmitted diseases became easily curable, and this, combined with effective public health campaigns against STIs, led to a public perception during the 1960s and 1970s that they have ceased to be a serious medical threat.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>During this period, the importance of contact tracing in treating STIs was recognized. By tracing the sexual partners of infected individuals, testing them for infection, treating the infected and tracing their contacts, in turn, STI clinics could effectively suppress infections in the general population.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In the 1980s, first genital herpes and then <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AIDS" title="AIDS">AIDS</a> emerged into the public consciousness as sexually transmitted diseases that could not be cured by modern medicine. AIDS, in particular, has a long asymptomatic period—during which time <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> (the human immunodeficiency virus, which causes AIDS) can replicate and the disease can be transmitted to others—followed by a symptomatic period, which leads rapidly to death unless treated. HIV/AIDS entered the United States from Haiti in about 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Recognition that AIDS threatened a global <a href="/wiki/Pandemic" title="Pandemic">pandemic</a> led to public information campaigns and the development of treatments that allow AIDS to be managed by suppressing the replication of HIV for as long as possible. Contact tracing continues to be an important measure, even when diseases are incurable, as it helps to contain infection.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

<li id="cite_note-GBD2015De-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-GBD2015De_4-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-GBD2015De_4-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFWangNaghaviAllenBarber2016">Wang H, Naghavi M, Allen C, Barber RM, Bhutta ZA,  et al.
